Confession of Price for Unreceived Slave
Ruling on conditional vs unconditional confession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a debtor confesses a debt from the price of an unreceived slave, distinguishing between specified and unspecified slaves.

Core Idea
Specification of the slave gives the creditor an option; without specification, the debt is binding.

This passage from al-Quduri presents a nuanced ruling about a confession of debt arising from a sale where the purchased slave has not been delivered. The outcome hinges on whether the slave is specifically identified or not—a distinction that affects the creditor's rights. Understanding this ruling sharpens your ability to analyze conditional and unconditional confessions in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the confession scenario
The confessor says: 'I owe you one thousand from the price of a slave I bought from you but did not take possession.' Identify the key elements: confession of debt, source of debt (sale price), and the fact that possession was not taken.
2
Check for specification of the slave
Examine whether the confessor specifies the slave (e.g., 'that particular slave') or leaves it general. The wording 'bi-ʿaynihi' indicates specification.
The phrase 'عَبْدًا بِعَيْنِهِ' means 'a specific slave'.
3
Apply the corresponding ruling
If specified, the confesse (creditor) has an option: either accept delivery of that slave and take the thousand, or forfeit the claim. If unspecified, the debt is binding according to Abu Hanifa—the thousand must be paid regardless of delivery.
The ruling for unspecified is given as 'lazimahu al-alf' (the thousand is binding) in Abu Hanifa's view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ قَالَ لَهُ عَلَيَّ أَلْفٌ مِنْ ثَمَن عَبْدِ اشْتَرَيْتِهِ مِنْهُ وَلَمْ أَقْبِضْهُ فَإِنْ ذَكَرَ عَبْدًا بِعَيْنِهِ قِيلَ لِلْمُقَرِ لَهُ إِنْ شِنْت فَسَلَّمَ الْعَبْدَ وَخُذْ الْأَلْفَ، وَإِلَّا فَلَا شَيْءَ لَكَ وَإِنْ قَالَ مِنْ ثَمَن عَبْدِ لَمْ يُعَيِّنْهُ إلَّا أَتي لَمْ أَقْبِضْهُ لَزِمَهُ الْأَلْفُ فِي قَوْلِ أَبِي حَنِيفَةَ
And if he says, 'I owe you one thousand from the price of a slave I bought from him and I have not taken possession,' then if he mentioned a specific slave, it is said to the confesse: if you wish, deliver the slave and take the thousand, otherwise you have nothing. And if he said from the price of a slave he did not specify, except that I have not taken possession, the thousand is binding according to Abu Hanifa.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This ruling deals with a confession of a debt arising from a sale where the slave has not been delivered. If the confessor specifies the slave (ʿabd bi-ʿaynihi), the confesse (muqarr lahu) has the option: either deliver that slave to complete the sale and take the thousand, or forfeit the claim. If the confessor does not specify the slave, Abu Hanifa holds that the thousand is binding as a debt because the unspecified reference does not create a conditional obligation; it is a simple confession of debt. This shows the importance of specification in conditional confessions.

Key Terms
بِعَيْنِهِ (bi-ʿaynihi): Specific, identified exactly.
إقرار (iqrār): Confession, admission of a right or debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, if someone confesses 'I owe you 1000 dirhams from the price of a slave I bought from you but did not take possession' without specifying the slave, what is the ruling?
A) The confession is invalid
B) He owes 1000 dirhams unconditionally
C) He owes nothing until he specifies
D) He owes the slave

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a confession of debt from an unreceived slave is binding; specifying the slave gives the creditor an option, but without specification, the debt is absolute.

Confession of Debt from Prohibited Sale
Ruling on confession of debt from prohibited sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a confession of debt is binding even if the underlying transaction is prohibited, and explain why the debtor's interpretation (tafsir) is not accepted.

Core Idea
A confession of debt is binding and cannot be escaped by claiming the debt arose from a forbidden transaction.

This lesson examines a concise ruling from Al-Quduri's Mukhtasar about a person who confesses to owing money from the sale of wine or a pig – items that are legally unsellable. The ruling clarifies that the confession itself creates a binding obligation, and the confessor cannot later excuse himself by revealing the unlawful origin of the debt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the confession scenario
The person says 'I owe you one thousand dirhams from the price of wine or pig.' Note that the debtor explicitly links the debt to a prohibited transaction.
Wine and pig are used as examples of items whose sale is haram.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The ruling is 'lazimahu al-alfu' – the thousand dirhams are binding. The confession creates a valid obligation.
The ruling does not depend on the validity of the underlying sale.
3
Note the rejection of tafsir
The debtor's explanation (tafsir) that the debt is from an illegal source is not accepted. He cannot use this to escape payment.
Tafsir here means his claim about the cause of the debt.
4
Understand the legal principle
A confession of obligation is enforceable regardless of the legality of the cause. The act of confessing a debt is separate from the transaction that gave rise to it.
This principle ensures legal certainty and prevents abuse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ قَالَ لَهُ عَلَيَّ أَلْفُ دِرْهَمٍ مِنْ ثَمَنِ خَمْرٍ، أَوْ خِنْزِيرٍ لَزِمَهُ الْأَلْفُ وَلَمْ يُقْبَلْ تَفْسِيرُهُ
And if he says, 'I owe him one thousand dirhams from the price of wine or pig,' the thousand is binding and his interpretation is not accepted.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This ruling addresses a confession where the debt is claimed to be from the price of wine or a pig – items that are not permissible to sell in Islam. Nevertheless, the confession is binding because the confessor is admitting a financial obligation. He cannot later claim that the debt was for a forbidden transaction to avoid payment, as that would be an attempt to nullify the confession. The Hanafi school holds that the validity of the underlying cause is separate from the fact of the confession. The confession creates a debt regardless of the legality of the consideration.

Key Terms
تفسيره (tafsīruhu): His explanation that the debt is from a prohibited source.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone confesses 'I owe you 1000 dirhams from the price of wine', according to the text, what happens?
A) The confession is void because wine is unlawful
B) He owes 1000 dirhams
C) He can explain it was a joke
D) He owes half

Key Takeaway
A confession of debt is binding and the debtor cannot avoid payment by claiming the debt originated from a forbidden transaction.

Iqrar of Pregnancy and Debts During Death Illness
Rulings on two distinct acknowledgments

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings on acknowledgment of pregnancy and debts during terminal illness, and apply the priority rule between health debts and illness debts.

Core Idea
An acknowledgment of pregnancy is valid; health debts are paid before those acknowledged during terminal illness.

This passage from Al-Quduri's matn addresses two distinct but related topics: the validity of acknowledging the pregnancy of owned animals or slaves, and the prioritization of debts when a person makes acknowledgments during final illness. Understanding these rulings is crucial for preserving the rights of heirs and creditors in end-of-life situations. The proofs from Quran and Sunnah reinforce these principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
Read the passage and separate the two main legal statements: (1) acknowledgment of pregnancy is valid and binding, (2) debts from health or known causes are given precedence over debts acknowledged during terminal illness without cause.
The passage uses 'wa' to connect the two topics.
2
Understand the scope of valid acknowledgment
The acknowledgment of pregnancy must be of a tangible, owned entity (slave-girl or sheep). The pregnancy must be known or knowable. The acknowledgment creates a right to the offspring upon birth.
This principle is derived from the hadith on newborn crying and inheriting.
3
Analyze the priority rule for debts
During terminal illness, debts that were incurred during health or from known causes during illness are paid first. If there are no such debts, the acknowledgment of debt during illness is valid and that creditor takes precedence over heirs.
The Quranic verse (4:12) and the hadith on paying debt before will support this.
4
Recognize the usul principles
The passage notes that the wording of acknowledgment must be clear; ambiguous acknowledgments are not valid. Also, rulings that apply after birth (like ownership) can be acknowledged during pregnancy, but those specific to the fetus itself (like inheritance) cannot be finalized until birth.
These principles guide the application of iqrar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِنْ أَقَرَّ بِحَمْلِ جَارِيَةٍ، أَوْ بِحَمْلِ شَاةٍ لِرَجُلٍ صَحَّ الْإِقْرَارُ وَلَزِمَهُ. وَإِذَا أَقَرَّ الرَّجُلُ فِي مَرَضِ مَوْتِهِ بِدُيُونٍ وَعَلَيْهِ دُيُونٌ لَزِمَتْهُ فِي صِحَتِهِ وَدُيُونٌ لَزِمَتْهُ فِي مَرَضِهِ بِأَسْبَابٍ مَعْلُومَةٍ فَدَيْنُ الصِّحَّةِ وَالدَّيْنُ الْمَعْرُوفُ بِالْأَسْبَابِ مُقَدَّمٌ فَإِذَا قُضِيَتْ وَإِنْ لَمْ يَكُنْ عَلَيْهِ دُيُونٌ لَزِمَتْهُ فِي صِحَتِهِ جَازَ إِقْرَارُهُ. وَكَانَ الْمُقَرُّ لَهُ أَوْلَى مِنَ الْوَرَثَةِ. وَجْهٌ (١) الْحَدِيثُ لِثُبُوتِ: عَنْ أَبِي هُرَيْرَةَ، عَنِ النَّبِيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم قَالَ: «إِذَا اسْتَهَلَّ الْمَوْلُودُ وُرِثَ» (سُنَنُ أَبِي دَاوُدَ، بَابٌ فِي الْمَوْلُودِ يَسْتَهِلُّ ثُمَّ يَمُوتُ، رقم ٢٩٢٠). وَجْهٌ (١) الْآيَةُ لِثُبُوتِ: ﴿فَإِن كَانُواْ أَكْثَرَ مِن ذَلِكَ فَهُمْ شُرَكَاءُ فِي الثُّلُثِ مِن بَعْدِ وَصِيَّةٍ يُوصَى بِهَا أَوْ دَيْنٍ غَيْرَ مُضَارٍّ﴾ (سورة النساء، ٤:١٢). وَجْهٌ (٢) الْحَدِيثُ لِثُبُوتِ: أَنَّ أَبَا هُرَيْرَةَ حَدَّثَهُ، أَنَّ رَسُولَ اللَّهِ صلى الله عليه وسلم قَالَ: «إِنَّ الرَّجُلَ لَيَعْمَلُ وَالْمَرْأَةُ بِطَاعَةِ اللَّهِ سِتِّينَ سَنَةً ثُمَّ يَحْضُرُهُمَا الْمَوْتُ فَيُضَارَّانِ فِي الْوَصِيَّةِ فَتَجِبُ لَهُمَا النَّارُ» (سُنَنُ أَبِي دَاوُدَ، بَابُ مَا جَاءَ فِي كَرَاهِيَةِ الْإِضْرَارِ فِي الْوَصِيَّةِ، رقم ٢٨٦٧). وَجْهٌ (١) الْآيَةُ لِثُبُوتِ وَكَانَ الْمُقَرُّ لَهُ أَوْلَى مِنَ الْوَرَثَةِ: ﴿فَإِن كَانُواْ أَكْثَرَ مِن ذَلِكَ فَهُمْ شُرَكَاءُ فِي الثُّلُثِ مِن بَعْدِ وَصِيَّةٍ يُوصَى بِهَا أَوْ دَيْنٍ غَيْرَ مُضَارٍّ﴾ (سورة النساء، ٤:١٢). وَجْهٌ (٢) الْحَدِيثُ لِثُبُوتِ وَكَانَ الْمُقَرُّ لَهُ أَوْلَى مِنَ الْوَرَثَةِ: عَنْ عَلِيٍّ، «أَنَّ النَّبِيَّ صلى الله عليه وسلم قَضَى بِالدَّيْنِ قَبْلَ الْوَصِيَّةِ» (سُنَنُ التِّرْمِذِيِّ، بَابُ مَا جَاءَ يُبْدَأُ بِالدَّيْنِ قَبْلَ الْوَصِيَّةِ، رقم ٢١٢٢). أُصُولٌ: لَفْظُ إِقْرَارٍ فِيهِ كَثِيرٌ فَلَا تَصْحِيحٌ إِلَّا بِرَفْعِ الشَّكِّ. أُصُولٌ: حَمْلُ الْأَحْكَامِ الَّتِي تَجْرِي بَعْدَ خُرُوجِ الْحَمْلِ جَائِزٌ إِقْرَارُهَا فِي الْحَمْلِ، وَمَا يَجْرِي فِي الْحَمْلِ نَفْسِهِ لَا يَجُوزُ.
And if he acknowledges the pregnancy of a slave-girl, or the pregnancy of a sheep for a man, the acknowledgment is valid and binding. And when a man acknowledges during his death-illness debts that were owed by him during his health and debts that became owed by him during his illness due to known causes, then the debt of health and the debt known by causes is given precedence. So when they are paid – and if there were no debts owed by him during his health – his acknowledgment is valid. And the one acknowledged for is more entitled than the heirs. Proof (1): the hadith for proof: from Abu Hurairah from the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'When a newborn baby cries out, he inherits.' (Sunan Abi Dawud). Proof (1): the verse for proof: 'But if they are more than that, then they are partners in one-third after any bequest he has made or debt, without harm' (Surah An-Nisa, 4:12). Proof (2): the hadith for proof: that Abu Hurairah narrated that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'Indeed a man and a woman may obey Allah for sixty years, then death approaches them and they cause harm in the will, so the Fire becomes obligatory for them' (Sunan Abi Dawud). Proof (1): the verse for proof of 'and the one acknowledged for is more entitled than the heirs': 'But if they are more than that, then they are partners in one-third after any bequest he has made or debt, without harm' (Surah An-Nisa, 4:12). Proof (2): the hadith for proof of 'and the one acknowledged for is more entitled than the heirs': from Ali that the Prophet (peace be upon him) decreed that debt is paid before the will (Sunan al-Tirmidhi). Principles: the word of acknowledgment has many aspects; it is only valid if the correct aspect is clarified. Principles: the acknowledgment of a pregnancy is valid for rulings that apply after the child is born; those that apply during pregnancy itself are not valid.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage from Al-Quduri's matn presents two distinct rulings within one numbered section. First, if a person acknowledges the pregnancy of a slave-girl or a sheep belonging to someone else, the acknowledgment is valid and binding. This is because the pregnancy is a tangible thing that can be owned, and the acknowledgment creates a right for the other person to the offspring once born. The hadith about the newborn crying supports the legal personality of the unborn in matters of inheritance, analogically applied here. Second, the passage addresses the situation of a person in terminal illness who acknowledges debts. The key point is the prioritization: debts incurred during health and debts with known causes during illness are given precedence over debts acknowledged without cause during illness. If the sick person has no health debts, his acknowledgment of debt during illness is valid and that creditor is given priority over the heirs. The proofs include Quranic verses on debt priority and hadiths warning against harmful wills and establishing debt before will. The usul notes remind us that the wording of acknowledgment must be clear; ambiguous acknowledgment is not valid. Also, rulings that apply after birth (like ownership) can be acknowledged during pregnancy, but rulings that apply to the fetus itself (like inheritance) cannot be finalized until birth.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment or confession of a right or obligation against oneself.
حمل (haml): Pregnancy, the fetus in the womb.
مرض الموت (marad al-mawt): Terminal illness, a sickness from which one expects death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the passage, which debt is given precedence in a terminal illness?
A) Debts acknowledged during the illness without any cause
B) Debts incurred during health or with known causes during illness
C) Debts to relatives only
D) Debts that are less than one-third of the estate

Key Takeaway
Acknowledgment of a known, existing right (like pregnancy) is valid; during terminal illness, prior debts take precedence over new acknowledgments.

The Immediate Claim of Shuf'a (Pre-emption)
Ruling on the necessity of claiming shuf'a in the same gathering

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when and how a pre-emptor must claim shuf'a, and distinguish the view of Imam Muhammad from the majority.

Core Idea
Shuf'a must be claimed immediately upon knowledge in the same gathering – delay risks loss of the right.

The right of pre-emption (shuf'a) protects co-owners from unwanted third-party intrusion. However, this right is not perpetual; the pre-emptor must act promptly. This passage from al-Quduri outlines the required procedure: upon learning of a sale, the pre-emptor must take witnesses in that very sitting, then formally notify the seller, buyer, or at the property. Imam Muhammad adds a one-month limit after the initial declaration, while Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf do not impose a strict time limit but require immediate action.

Guided Path
1
Identify the trigger
What event activates the right of shuf'a? The passage begins with 'When the pre-emptor learns of the sale' – knowledge of the sale is the trigger.
2
Specify the immediate action
What must the pre-emptor do in the same gathering? He must take witnesses to his claim (أَشْهَدَ فِي مَجْلِسِهِ ذَلِكَ عَلَى الْمُطَالَبَةِ).
The claim must be declared in the same sitting; leaving without declaration may forfeit the right.
3
Trace the subsequent steps
After the initial declaration, the pre-emptor proceeds to take witnesses against the seller (if he still holds the property), the buyer, or at the property itself. This formalizes the claim.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi difference
Imam Muhammad adds that if the pre-emptor delays more than one month after taking witnesses, the right becomes void. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf do not set a fixed time limit, but the claim must not be abandoned.
The mufta bihi follows Abu Hanifa, but the month limit is a cautionary view.
5
Understand the consequence of omission
If the pre-emptor fails to take witnesses immediately or delays excessively, his right of shuf'a is invalidated (بَطَلَتْ).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا عَلِمَ الشَّفِيعُ بِالْبَيْعِ أَشْهَدَ فِي مَجْلِسِهِ ذَلِكَ عَلَى الْمُطَالَبَةِ ثُمَّ يَنْهَضُ مِنْهُ فَيُشْهِدُ عَلَى الْبَائِعِ إِنْ كَانَ الْمَبِيعُ فِي يَدِهِ أَوْ عَلَى الْمُبْتَاعِ، أَوْ عِنْدَ الْعَقَارِ وَقَالَ مُحَمَّدٌ إِنْ تَرَكَهَا شَهْرًا بَعْدَ الْإِشْهَادِ بَطَلَتْ
When the pre-emptor learns of the sale, he takes witnesses in that same gathering to his claim, then he proceeds from there to take witnesses against the seller if the property is in his hand, or against the buyer, or at the property. And Muhammad said: If he leaves it for a month after taking witnesses, it becomes void.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage states the Hanafi ruling on the timely claim of shuf'a. According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, the pre-emptor must declare his claim as soon as he learns of the sale, in the same sitting. He should announce his claim before witnesses, then formally assert it against the seller, buyer, or at the property itself. Imam Muhammad adds that if he delays for a month after that initial declaration, his right is lost. This is an intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf do not impose a specific time limit after the declaration, but Imam Muhammad's view gives a maximum period.

Key Terms
شُفْعَة (shuf‘a): The right of pre-emption; the right of a co-owner to acquire the share of another co-owner when sold to a third party.
شَّفِيع (shafī‘): The pre-emptor; the person entitled to claim shuf'a.
مُطَالَبَة (muṭālabah): The legal claim or demand for shuf'a.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What must the pre-emptor do immediately upon learning of the sale according to this passage?
A) Wait until the buyer takes possession
B) Take witnesses to his claim in the same gathering
C) Inform the seller privately
D) Begin negotiations with the buyer

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school demands that shuf'a be claimed immediately upon knowledge in the same sitting, with witnesses, to preserve the right.

Applicability of Shuf'a to All Real Estate
Ruling statement: shuf'a is wajib in real estate, even when indivisible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that shuf'a is established in all real estate and explain why indivisibility does not negate the right of pre-emption.

Core Idea
Shuf'a is established in all real estate, even where the property cannot be physically divided.

This lesson establishes a foundational principle of Hanafi sales law: the right of pre-emption (shuf'a) is not limited to divisible properties. The text from al-Quduri is concise, but it resolves a potential ambiguity—students often wonder whether a small, indivisible plot triggers shuf'a. The answer is yes, and the reason lies in the nature of shuf'a as a right attached to co-ownership in immovable property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the exercise of shuf'a (right of pre-emption). The text says it is 'wājib' (established/confirmed). In Hanafi fiqh, wājib here denotes a legally enforceable right, not a religious obligation.
Shuf'a is not a ritual obligation but a property right.
2
Define the scope
The phrase 'fī al-ʿaqār' (in real estate) limits shuf'a to immovable property. Movable goods, such as cars or livestock, are excluded.
Real estate includes land, buildings, and other fixed property.
3
Note the exception
The phrase 'wa in kāna mimmā lā yuqsam' (even if it is of the type that cannot be divided) clarifies that indivisibility does not cancel shuf'a. This refutes a potential misunderstanding that shuf'a only applies to partitionable properties.
Indivisible means the property cannot be physically split without significant loss or damage.
4
Understand the legal implication
Since shuf'a applies even to indivisible shares, a co-owner cannot avoid pre-emption by claiming that the property is too small to divide. The right attaches to the share itself, regardless of partition feasibility.
The ruling is unanimous among Hanafis and recorded in Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالشُّفْعَةُ وَاجِبَةٌ فِي الْعَقَارِ، وَإِنْ كَانَ مِمَّا لَا يُقْسَمُ
And shuf'a is obligatory in real estate, even if it is of the type that cannot be divided.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This is a fundamental principle of Hanafi law: shuf'a applies only to immovable property (real estate) and it applies regardless of whether the property can be physically divided among co-owners. This means even if the property is such that each shareholder's share cannot be separately demarcated (e.g., a very small plot), the right of pre-emption still exists.

Key Terms
عَقَار (‘aqār): Real estate; immovable property such as land and buildings.
لا يُقْسَم (lā yuqsam): Indivisible; the property cannot be physically partitioned without loss or devaluation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Does shuf'a apply to real estate that is too small to be divided?
A) Yes, according to Hanafis
B) No, only if divisible
C) Only if the buyer agrees
D) Only if the seller allows

Key Takeaway
Shuf'a is a confirmed right in all real estate, including properties that cannot be physically partitioned.

Scope of Shuf'a: Movables, Religious Status, Consideration
Three rulings delimiting shuf'a scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions required for shuf'a according to the Hanafi school and explain why movables and ships are excluded.

Core Idea
Shuf'a is only for real estate acquired by a material exchange, and religion does not affect the right.

This passage from the Quduri defines the boundaries of the right of pre-emption (shuf'a). It clarifies that shuf'a is not a general property right but limited to real estate transactions involving a material consideration, and it applies equally to Muslims and dhimmis. Understanding these limits prevents over-application of shuf'a.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three rulings
The passage contains three separate legal statements. List each one explicitly.
Memorize the three: movables/ships, equality, material compensation.
2
Understand the exclusion of movables and ships
Shuf'a applies only to real estate (aqar). Movables and ships are not real estate, even if ship docks.
Think of ships as legally movable, like cars.
3
Examine the condition of material consideration
Shuf'a is only established when real estate is acquired for a compensation that is wealth (mal).
Gifts and inheritances are excluded.
4
Apply the equality rule
Muslim and dhimmi have equal shuf'a rights; religion does not matter.
This is specific to Hanafi school; some other schools differ.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلا شُفْعَةَ فِي الْعُرُوضِ وَلا فِي السُّفُنِ وَالْمُسْلِمُ وَالذِّمِّيُّ فِي الشُّفْعَةِ سَوَاءٌ وَإِذَا مَلَكَ الْعَقَارَ بِعِوَضٍ هُوَ مَالٌ وَجَبَتْ فِيهِ الشُّفْعَةُ
And there is no shuf'a in movables nor in ships. And the Muslim and the dhimmi are equal with respect to shuf'a. And when one takes ownership of real estate for a compensation that is wealth, shuf'a becomes obligatory in it.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage clarifies the scope of shuf'a. First, shuf'a does not apply to movable goods (urūḍ) or to ships, which are considered movable in Hanafi law despite their size. Second, both Muslim and dhimmi (a non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state) have equal rights to claim shuf'a; religious status does not affect this right. Third, shuf'a is only established when real estate is acquired through a transaction that involves a material compensation (i.e., a price in wealth), such as a sale. Transactions without a material exchange, like inheritance or gift, do not give rise to shuf'a.

Key Terms
عُرُوض (‘urūḍ): Movable goods; chattels, as opposed to real estate.
سُفُن (sufun): Ships; considered movable property for shuf'a purposes.
ذِمِّيّ (dhimmī): A non-Muslim citizen living under Muslim rule with a contract of protection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) No, only Muslims
C) Only if the property is in a Muslim-majority area
D) Only if the seller is also a dhimmi

Key Takeaway
Shuf'a is limited to real estate acquired through a material exchange, and all owners, Muslim or dhimmi, have equal rights.

Transactions Excluded from Shuf'a
Enumeration of non-sale transfers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the specific transactions that do not trigger shuf'a and explain why settlement with admission (iqrar) is treated differently.

Core Idea
Shuf'a only arises from sales; non-sale transfers like dower, khulu', rent, and settlement with denial do not give shuf'a.

This passage from Al-Quduri delineates the boundaries of shuf'a (pre-emption) rights in Hanafi fiqh. It enumerates transactions that involve the transfer of real estate but do not create a right of pre-emption because they are not considered sales or equivalent exchanges. Understanding this list is crucial for correctly applying shuf'a rules in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base rule
Shuf'a is a right to replace the buyer in a sale of real estate. The passage first clarifies that not every transfer triggers this right.
The base rule is that shuf'a attaches only to sales (and equivalents).
2
List the excluded transactions
The passage enumerates six specific transactions: marriage dower (mahr), khulu‘ (divorce by compensation), rental (ijara), compensation for intentional homicide (diyat), freeing a slave for compensation, and settlement (sulh) with denial or silence.
Each of these is a valid transfer of property but does not count as a sale for shuf'a purposes.
3
Identify the exception
Settlement with admission (iqrar) is treated as a sale because the admitter essentially acknowledges the other's right and then buys it back.
The key distinction is whether the settlement involves an admission (iqrar) or a denial (inkar).
4
Understand the rationale
The Hanafi school restricts shuf'a to transfers that are purely exchanges of property for a definite price. In dower, khulu', diyat, etc., the consideration is either not wealth (e.g., marital rights) or is not a clear price.
This is a matter of ta'lil (ratio legis).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلا شُفْعَةَ فِي الدَّارِ يَتَزَوَّجُ الرَّجُلُ عَلَيْهَا، أَوْ يُخَالِعُ امْرَأَتَهُ بِمَا أَوْ يَسْتَأْجِرُ بِمَا دَارًا، أَوْ يُصَالِحُ بِمَا عَنْ دَمِ عَمْدٍ أَوْ يُعْتِقُ عَلَيْهَا عَبْدًا أَوْ يُصَالِحُ عَنْهَا بِإِنْكَارٍ، أَوْ سُكُوتِه فَإِنْ صَالَحَ عَنْهَا بِإِقْرَارِ وَجَبَتْ فِيهَا الشُّفْعَةُ
And there is no shuf'a in the house regarding which a man marries a woman (by making it the dower), or by which he divorces his wife in khulu‘, or by which he rents a house, or by which he makes a settlement for intentional homicide, or by which he frees a slave, or by which he makes a settlement with denial or his silence. If he makes a settlement with acknowledgement, shuf'a becomes obligatory in it.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage enumerates transactions that transfer ownership of real estate but do not create a right of pre-emption because they are not considered sales or equivalent exchanges. These include: marriage dower (mahr), khulu‘ (divorce by compensation), rental, diyat (blood money) for intentional killing, freeing a slave in exchange, and sulh (settlement) where the defendant denies the claim or remains silent. However, if the settlement is based on an admission (iqrār) of the claim, then it is treated like a sale and shuf'a applies. The Hanafi school distinguishes these based on the nature of the consideration: shuf'a only attaches to a transfer in exchange for a definite compensation (thaman) that is wealth.

Key Terms
يُخَالِع (yukhāli‘u): Khulu‘: a form of divorce where the wife gives compensation to the husband to obtain separation.
يُصَالِح (yuṣāliḥu): To make a settlement; a compromise agreement, often used in lieu of litigation.
إِقْرَار (iqrār): Admission or acknowledgment of a right or claim.
إِنْكَار (inkār): Denial of a claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of settlement (sulh) triggers shuf'a according to this passage?
A) Settlement with denial (inkār)
B) Settlement with silence (sukūt)
C) Settlement with admission (iqrār)
D) Settlement with free will

Key Takeaway
Shuf'a only applies to sales and sale-equivalent contracts, not to transfers like dower, khulu', rent, diyat, manumission, or settlement without admission.

Pre-emption Litigation: Procedure and Options
States permissibility of pre-emption litigation without price, and shafi' options

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on pre-emption litigation without depositing the price and list the shafi' options available after the decree.

Core Idea
The shafi' may sue without bringing the price; payment is due only after the decree.

This passage from al-Quduri’s matn addresses a practical procedural question: must the pre-emptor (shafi‘) deposit the purchase price when initiating litigation? The text clarifies that he need not; the price obligation arises only after a favorable decree. Additionally, the shafi‘ retains the standard buyer’s options to rescind for defects or unseen features.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its permissibility
The text begins with 'tajūzu al-munāza'atu fī al-shuf'ah' – litigation in pre-emption is permissible. Understand that this is the baseline ruling.
The permissibility includes all forms of legal proceedings.
2
Note the condition waived initially
The shafi' does not have to bring the purchase price to court at the time of filing. This is a concession to facilitate the right of pre-emption.
The phrase 'wa in lam yuḥḍir al-thaman' indicates this waiver.
3
Recognize the triggering of the payment obligation
Once the judge decrees in favor of the shafi', producing the price becomes wājib (obligatory). The sequence is: decree → obligation to pay.
The text: 'fa-idhā qaḍā lahu al-qāḍī bil-shuf'ati lazimahu iḥḍāru al-thaman'.
4
Identify the retained options
After acquiring the property via shuf'ah, the shafi' still has the right to return it if a hidden defect is discovered (khiyār al-‘ayb) or if he had not seen the property beforehand and is dissatisfied (khiyār al-ru’yah).
These are the same options the buyer would have in a normal sale.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَتَجُوزُ الْمُنَازَعَةُ فِي الشُّفْعَةِ، وَإِنْ لَمْ يُحْضِرُ الشَّفِيعُ الثَّمَنَ إِلَى تَجْلِسِ الْقَاضِي فَإِذَا قَضَى لَهُ الْقَاضِي بِالشُّفْعَةِ لَزِمَهُ إِحْضَارُ الثَّمَنِ وَلِلشَّفِيعِ أَنْ يَرُدَّ الدَّارَ بِخِيَارِ الْعَيْبِ وَالرُّؤْيَةِ
Litigation in pre-emption is permissible even if the pre-emptor does not bring the price to the court of the judge. Then when the judge decrees pre-emption for him, it becomes obligatory for him to produce the price. And the pre-emptor has the right to return the property due to option of defect and option of inspection.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from al-Quduri's matn. It clarifies the procedure: the shafi' may start court proceedings without depositing the purchase price. This is a concession because the price may be disputed or the shafi' may not have it at hand. However, once the judge upholds his right, he must pay. Additionally, the shafi' retains standard buyer's options: if the property is defective or he had not seen it and is dissatisfied, he may rescind.

Key Terms
الشُّفْعَة (al-shuf'ah): Right of pre-emption allowing a co-owner or neighbor to replace the buyer in a sale.
خِيَارُ الْعَيْبِ (khiyar al-'ayb): Option to annul sale due to a hidden defect.
خِيَارُ الرُّؤْيَةِ (khiyar al-ru'yah): Option to rescind after seeing an item bought unseen.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When must the shafi' produce the purchase price?
A) Before filing the lawsuit.
B) Immediately after the sale.
C) Only after the judge decrees in his favor.
D) At any time within three days.

Key Takeaway
A shafi' can initiate pre-emption litigation without immediate payment; the price must be paid only after the judge's decree; and he retains the right to rescind for defects or unseen condition.

Loss of Shuf'ah: Five Grounds of Nullification
Enumerates actions that nullify the pre-emptor's right

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five actions that nullify the right of shuf'ah in Hanafi fiqh and explain the reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
Your right of pre-emption is lost if you delay, sell, die, or take payment — it is personal and must be exercised immediately.

The right of shuf'ah is a powerful but delicate claim. The Hanafi school specifies several ways this right is voided, emphasizing the need for immediate action and personal assertion. In this lesson, we examine the five situations enumerated by al-Quduri where the pre-emptor's claim is nullified.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first ground — failure to demand
The pre-emptor must formally demand (ishhad) immediately upon knowledge. If he fails while able, the right is void because silence indicates abandonment.
The demand must be in front of witnesses; mere mental intention is insufficient.
2
Distinguish between taking compensation and legitimate waiver
If the pre-emptor accepts payment in exchange for his right, the right is void and he must return the compensation. This is not a valid sale of the right but an invalid condition.
Taking compensation is considered an implicit waiver; the right is not a commodity to be sold.
3
Recognize the personal nature of shuf'ah
The right is not inherited. If the pre-emptor dies, the right dies with him. But if the buyer dies, the right continues against his heirs.
This asymmetry highlights that the right attaches to the property, not to the person of the buyer.
4
Understand the effect of selling the property claim
If the pre-emptor sells the property (the subject of pre-emption) before judgment, the pre-emption is void because he has acted inconsistently with his claim.
Selling the property implies the pre-emptor no longer wishes to have it himself.
5
Synthesize the underlying principle
All five grounds show that shuf'ah is a right that requires immediate assertion and personal maintenance. Any act of delay, sale, death, or acceptance of payment defeats it.
The common rationale is that the right is intended to prevent harm, but hesitation or transfer indicates consent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا تَرَكَ الشَّفِيعُ الإِشْهَادَ حِينَ عَلِمَ وَهُوَ يَقْدِرُ عَلَى ذَلِكَ بَطَلَتْ شُفْعَتُهُ فَإِنْ صَاحَ مِنْ شُفْعَتِهِ عَلَى عِوَضٍ أَخَذَهُ بَطَلَتْ شُفْعَتُهُ وَرَدَّ الْعِوَضَ وَإِذَا مَاتَ الشَّفِيعُ بَطَلَتْ شُفْعَتُهُ وَإِذَا مَاتَ الْمُشْتَرِي لَمْ تَسْقُطْ وَإِذَا بَاعَ الشَّفِيعُ مَا يَشْفَعُ بِهِ قَبْلَ أَنْ يُقْضَى لَهُ بِالشُّفْعَةِ بَطَلَتْ
And if the pre-emptor fails to bear witness [to his claim] when he learns and is able to do that, his pre-emption is void. If he buys out his right of pre-emption for compensation and takes it, his pre-emption is void, and he must return the compensation. And if the pre-emptor dies, his pre-emption is void. If the buyer dies, the pre-emption does not lapse. And if the pre-emptor sells what he has the right to pre-empt before judgement is given in his favor, it is void.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This ruling statement lists five ways the right of shuf'ah is lost. First, failing to formally demand (by having witnesses) when able—shows disinterest. Second, accepting payment in exchange for waiving the right—treats it as a contract, voiding the right. Third, death of the shafi'—right is personal, not inherited. Fourth, death of buyer does not cancel the right; it passes to buyer's heirs. Fifth, selling the subject property before verdict—implies abandonment.

Key Terms
الإِشْهَاد (al-ishhād): Formal witnessing of the claim to pre-emption, usually before witnesses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to the right of shuf'ah if the original buyer dies?
A) It is void.
B) It remains, against the buyer's heirs.
C) It transfers to the seller.
D) It is suspended for three days.

Key Takeaway
The right of shuf'ah is personal and must be asserted immediately; any delay, sale, death, or compensation voids it.

Agent's Right of Pre-emption and Sales with Option
Rulings on agency and option affecting shuf'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rules for when the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah) is established or lost in cases of agency and option clauses.

Core Idea
An agent that sells for the principal forfeits his own pre-emption; a buyer's agent retains it. Seller's option suspends, buyer's option hastens.

This passage from Al-Quduri's Mukhtasar provides clear rulings on three scenarios: when the seller's agent is also a pre-emptor, when the buyer's agent is a pre-emptor, and when the sale includes an option (khiyar) for either party. Understanding these rules is essential for correctly applying the law of pre-emption in real estate transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the party acting
Determine whether the person involved in the sale is the seller's agent, the buyer's agent, or the seller/buyer themselves.
2
Determine if the agent is the shafi'
If the agent is also a pre-emptor (shafi'), the ruling differs based on which side they represent.
3
Analyze the effect of option clauses
Check whether the seller or buyer has a stipulated option (khiyar), and whether it is still active or has been dropped.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَوَكِيلُ الْبَائِعِ إِذَا بَاعَ وَهُوَ شَفِيعٌ فَلَا شُفْعَةَ لَهُ وَكَذَلِكَ إِنْ ضَمِنَ الدَّرَكَ عَنْ الْبَائِعِ لِلْمُشْتَرِي وَوَكِيلُ الْمُشْتَرِي إِذَا ابْتَاعَ وَهُوَ شَفِيعٌ فَلَهُ الشُّفْعَةُ وَمَنْ بَاعَ بِشَرْطِ الْخِيَارِ فَلَا شُفْعَةَ لِلشَّفِيعِ فَإِنْ أَسْقَطَ الْخِيَارَ وَجَبَتْ الشُّفْعَةُ وَمَنْ اشْتَرَى بِشَرْطِ الْخِيَارِ وَجَبَتْ الشُّفْعَةُ
And an agent of the seller—if he sells, and he himself is a pre-emptor, he has no right of pre-emption. Similarly, if he guarantees against defects (daman al-darak) on behalf of the seller to the buyer [he has no right]. And an agent of the buyer—if he buys, and he is a pre-emptor, he has the right of pre-emption. And whoever sells with a condition of option [for the seller]—the pre-emptor has no right; if the option is dropped, the right becomes established. And whoever buys with a condition of option [for the buyer]—the right is established.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage addresses three juristic issues. First, when the seller's agent is also a pre-emptor, his act of selling (even for the principal) is treated as an act against his own right—he impliedly forfeits shuf'ah. Similarly, if he guarantees against defects (daman al-darak) for the buyer, he acts in opposition to his potential claim. Conversely, the buyer's agent retains his right as a separate party. Second, if the seller stipulates an option (khiyar al-shart) for himself, the sale is not final—if the shafi' takes, the seller might cancel, so shuf'ah is suspended until the option is dropped. Third, if the buyer has the option, the sale is binding on the seller, so shuf'ah is immediate.

Key Terms
ضَمِنَ الدَّرَك (daman al-darak): Guarantee by the seller (or agent) to compensate the buyer if the property is repossessed by a prior claimant, effectively a warranty.
الْخِيَار (al-khiyār): Option; a clause in a sale contract allowing one party to rescind within a specified period.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the seller stipulates an option for himself, can the shafi' immediately exercise pre-emption?
A) Yes, immediately.
B) No, not until the option expires or is dropped.
C) Yes, but only if he pays the price.
D) No, he loses his right forever.

Key Takeaway
The right of pre-emption is affected by agency and option clauses: the seller's agent forfeits it, the buyer's agent retains it, and a seller's option delays it until removed.

Shuf'ah in Invalid Sales, Dhimmi Transactions, and Gifts
Rulings on shuf'ah in three distinct scenarios

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for shuf'ah in invalid sales, dhimmi transactions with prohibited items, and gifts, and apply them to new cases.

Core Idea
Shuf'ah requires a valid sale; invalid sales, dhimmi wine/pork deals, and gifts have special rules.

This lesson distills three distinct shuf'ah rules from the matn. Each flows from a single principle: shuf'ah is a right attached to a valid exchange. When the sale is invalid, no shuf'ah arises until the defect is cured. When dhimmis transact using wine or pork, the pre-emptor's status determines the price. And gifts generally carry no shuf'ah unless they are compensated transfers. Understanding these three cases sharpens your ability to identify when shuf'ah applies in everyday transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three cases
The passage presents three distinct rulings: (1) invalid purchase and shuf'ah, (2) dhimmi buying with wine/pork, (3) gift. Each has a condition that triggers or blocks shuf'ah.
These are separate but linked by the theme of shuf'ah.
2
Extract the ruling for invalid sale
No shuf'ah in an invalid sale (fasid). But if the invalidity is removed (e.g., by fulfilling the missing condition), shuf'ah becomes obligatory because the impediment is gone.
The right of shuf'ah is not born until the sale is valid.
3
Extract the ruling for dhimmi transactions
When two dhimmis trade a house for wine or pork, and the pre-emptor is also dhimmi, he takes the house in exchange for the same wine/pork (lawful for them). If the pre-emptor is Muslim, he must pay the monetary value, as he cannot own those impure items.
The ruling respects the religious status of the parties.
4
Extract the ruling for gifts
No shuf'ah in a gift (hiba) unless it is a gift with a stipulated compensation (hiba bi-'iwad). Then it is treated like a sale, and shuf'ah applies.
A pure gift is not a commercial exchange, so no shuf'ah.
5
Summarize the common thread
Shuf'ah attaches only to binding exchanges that are equivalent to a valid sale. Invalid sales, non-compensatory gifts, and transactions with impure consideration for Muslims all block or modify shuf'ah.
This principle helps qadis and muftis determine shuf'ah in novel cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمَنْ ابْتَاعَ دَارًا شِرَاءً فَاسِدًا فَلَا شُفْعَةَ فِيهَا فَإِنْ أَسْقَطَ الْفَسْخَ وَجَبَتْ الشُّفْعَةُ لِزَوَالِ الْمَانِعِ وَإِذَا اشْتَرَى ذِمِيٌّ مِنْ ذِمّي دَارًا بِخَمْرٍ أَوْ خِنْزِيرٍ وَشَفِيعُهَا ذِمِّي أَخَذَهَا بِمِثْلِ الْخَمْرِ وَإِنْ كَانَ شَفِيعُهَا مُسْلِمًا أَخَذَهَا بِقِيمَةِ الْخَمْرِ وَالْخِنْزِيرِ وَلَا شُفْعَةَ فِي الْهِبَةِ إِلَّا أَنْ تَكُونَ بِعِوَضٍ مَشْرُوطٍ
And whoever buys a house with an invalid purchase—there is no pre-emption in it; if he removes the invalidity, pre-emption becomes obligatory due to removal of the impediment. And when a dhimmi buys from a dhimmi a house with wine or pork, and its pre-emptor is a dhimmi, he takes it with the like of wine/pork; if its pre-emptor is a Muslim, he takes it with the value of wine and pork. And there is no pre-emption in a gift unless it is with a stipulated compensation.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage covers three distinct cases. First, an invalid sale (e.g., lacking a condition of validity) does not create a right of shuf'ah because the sale itself is not binding. If the parties subsequently validate the sale (e.g., by removing the defect), the right emerges. Second, when dhimmis (non-Muslim subjects) transact using prohibited items like wine or pork as price, and the pre-emptor is also dhimmi, he takes the property in exchange for the same wine/pork—since it is lawful for them. But if the pre-emptor is Muslim, he must pay the monetary value of those items, as he cannot own them. Third, gifts (hiba) generally do not give shuf'ah because they are not sales; however, if the gift is conditional upon compensation (hiba bi-'iwad), it is treated like a sale, establishing shuf'ah.

Key Terms
شِرَاءً فَاسِدًا (shirā'an fāsidā): A sale that is defective but not void; it lacks some condition for validity but may be corrected.
ذِمِّي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim subject of an Islamic state who pays jizya and enjoys protection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, when does shuf'ah arise in an invalid purchase?
A) Immediately, because the buyer has possession.
B) Only after the invalidity is removed (e.g., defect cured).
C) Never, because shuf'ah requires a valid sale from the outset.
D) Only if the seller and buyer agree to it.

Key Takeaway
Shuf'ah attaches only to valid compensating transfers; invalid sales, dhimmi transactions with impure items, and pure gifts require careful application of conditions.

Resolving Price Disputes in Pre-emption (Shuf'ah)
Procedural rules for price disputes in shuf'ah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi rules for determining the price a shafi' must pay when there is a dispute over the price.

Core Idea
The buyer's word is default, but evidence shifts priority; payment changes the binding price.

When a pre-emptor (shafi’) exercises his right of shuf'ah, disputes may arise about the actual purchase price. The Hanafi school provides clear procedural rules: first between the shafi' and the buyer, and second between the buyer and the seller. This lesson unpacks these rules from Al-Quduri's matn, showing how evidence and payment status affect the outcome.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main dispute scenarios
The passage covers disputes between shafi' and buyer (scenarios 1 & 2) and between buyer and seller (scenario 3). Recognize these as separate legal problems with different default rules.
2
Apply the default rule for shafi' vs buyer without evidence
When only statements exist, the buyer's word is trusted because he actually paid the price. The shafi' must accept the buyer's claimed price.
This is the first sentence: 'al-qawlu qawlu al-mushtarī'.
3
Apply the rule when both produce evidence
If both bring witnesses, the shafi'’s evidence takes precedence per Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. The shafi' is the one claiming the right, so his proof is stronger.
Note 'indahumā' refers to the two companions (Sahibayn).
4
Apply the rule when buyer and seller dispute the price
If the buyer says a higher price and the seller says lower, and the price is unpaid, the shafi' takes at the seller's (lower) word. If already paid, he must pay the buyer's (higher) claim.
Payment is decisive because it reflects the actual transaction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا اخْتَلَفَ الشَّفِيعُ، وَالْمُشْتَرِي فِي الثَّمَنِ فَالْقَوْلُ قَوْلُ الْمُشْتَرِي فَإِنْ أَقَامَا بَيِّنَةً فَالْبَيِّنَةُ بَيِّنَةُ الشَّفِيعِ عِنْدَهُمَا وَإِذَا ادَّعَى الْمُشْتَرِي ثَمَنًا وَادَّعَى الْبَائِعٌ أَقَلَّ مِنْهُ وَلَمْ يَقْبِضُ الثَّمَنَ أَخَذَهَا الشَّفِيعُ بِمَا قَالَ الْبَائِعُ وَإِنْ كَانَ قَبَضَ الثَّمَنَ أَخَذَهَا بِمَا قَالَ الْمُشْتَرِي إِنْ شَاءَ وَلَمْ يَلْتَفِتْ إِلَى قَوْلِ الْبَائِعِ
And if the pre-emptor and the buyer dispute about the price, the statement is the statement of the buyer. If they both produce evidence, the evidence of the pre-emptor is given precedence according to the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad]. And if the buyer claims a certain price, and the seller claims a lesser amount, and the price has not been paid, the pre-emptor takes it for what the seller said; if the price has been paid, he takes it for what the buyer said, if he wishes, and does not pay attention to the seller's statement.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage provides rules for price disputes in shuf'ah. First, between shafi' and buyer: the buyer's word is accepted (since he is the actual payer). But if both bring witnesses, the shafi' witnesses prevail—per the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Second, if the buyer and seller disagree on the price (buyer says higher, seller says lower), and the price hasn't been paid, the shafi' takes at the lower price (selling party's word) because it favors the shafi'. If the buyer has already paid, the shafi' must pay the higher amount claimed by buyer, as payment indicates the real price.

Key Terms
الْبَيِّنَةُ بَيِّنَةُ الشَّفِيعِ (al-bayyina bayyinatu al-shafī'i): The evidence of the pre-emptor is given priority over the buyer's evidence if both produce it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the buyer claims the price was 1000, the seller claims it was 800, and the price has already been paid, what will the shafi' pay?
A) 800
B) 1000
C) 900
D) The amount he chooses

Key Takeaway
In shuf'ah price disputes, the default reliance is on the buyer's word, but evidence and payment status can override it to determine the final price.

Pre-emptor's Liability for Price Reductions and Increases
Effect of price reduction or increase after sale on the shafi'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the three Hanafi rules governing how post-sale price adjustments affect the shafi's payment obligation.

Core Idea
Partial reduction benefits the shafi'; full reduction does not; voluntary increase does not bind him.

This lesson examines three distinct rulings from the matn regarding post-sale adjustments between seller and buyer and their impact on the pre-emptor (shafi'). Understanding these rules prevents manipulation of preemption rights through price changes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
The passage presents three cases: partial reduction, full reduction, and increase. Name each scenario in your own words.
2
Analyze the effect on the shafi'
For each scenario, determine whether the shafi' benefits from the adjustment or not. Pay attention to the language: 'saqaṭa' (falls) vs 'lam yasquṭ' (does not fall).
3
Understand the rationale
Reflect on why a full reduction is treated differently from a partial one. The purpose is to prevent fraud and collusion between seller and buyer.
4
Apply to a new situation
Given a specific price adjustment, state how much the shafi' must pay. Practice with the checkpoint scenario.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا حَطَّ الْبَائِعُ عَنْ الْمُشْتَرِي بَعْضَ الثَّمَنِ سَقَطَ ذَلِكَ عَنْ الشَّفِيعِ وَإِنْ حَطَّ عَنْهُ جَمِيعَ الثَّمَنِ لَمْ يَسْقُطْ عَنْ الشَّفِيعِ وَإِنْ زَادَ الْمُشْتَرِي الْبَائِعَ فِي الثَّمَنِ لَمْ تَلْزَمُ الزِّيَادَةُ الشَّفِيعَ
And if the seller reduces part of the price for the buyer, that reduction is deducted from the pre-emptor. And if he reduces the entire price for him, it is not deducted from the pre-emptor. And if the buyer increases the price for the seller, the increase does not bind the pre-emptor.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage addresses post-sale adjustments. If the seller voluntarily forgives part of the price (ibtāl or ḥaṭṭ), the shafi' benefits from the reduction—he pays less. However, if the seller forgives the entire price (making it a gift of the property), the shafi' cannot take it for free; he still must pay the original price? The text says 'lam yasquṭ' (it does not fall), meaning he is not relieved of the price. This prevents the seller and buyer from colluding to deprive the shafi'. Conversely, if the buyer voluntarily adds to the price, the shafi' is not forced to pay the extra—he pays the original price. The purpose is to prevent manipulation of the shafi' right.

Key Terms
حَطَّ (ḥaṭṭa): To remit or reduce a debt or price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the seller reduces the price from 1000 to 500 after the sale, how much must the shafi' pay?
A) 1000
B) 500
C) His choice between 500 and 1000
D) Nothing

Key Takeaway
Partial reductions pass to the shafi', but full forgiveness does not; voluntary increases do not bind the shafi'.

Multiple Pre-emptors and Payment Scenarios
Rulings on multiple pre-emptors, payment types, and mistaken price waiver

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state how pre-emption is divided among multiple claimants, determine the payment method based on the type of price, and evaluate the validity of a waiver when the reported price is mistaken.

Core Idea
Pre-emptors share equally per head, not by ownership; payment method depends on the price type; mistaken low price revokes waiver.

This passage from Al-Quduri's matn lays down three key rules of shuf'ah: the distribution among co-pre-emptors, the method of paying for the property based on what the buyer paid, and the consequences of a pre-emptor waiving his right based on a mistaken price report. Understanding these rules is essential for correctly applying the law of pre-emption in real estate transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule for multiple pre-emptors
State the Hanafi rule: all pre-emptors share equally regardless of their ownership shares.
Text: 'al-shuf'atu baynahum 'alā 'adadi ru'ūsihim bil-sawiyya'.
2
Classify payment methods for different price types
Distinguish between 'araḍ (commodity), makīl/mawzūn (measured/weighed), and real estate exchange, and state the corresponding payment.
'Araḍ → qīma (market value); makīl/mawzūn → mithl (same quantity); real estate → value of each.
3
Analyze the mistaken price scenario – lower price
Explain why a waiver given upon hearing a higher price is void if the actual price is lower or different in kind.
The shafi' was induced to waive by misinformation; the waiver is not valid.
4
Contrast the dinar case (equal or higher value)
Explain why a waiver stands if the actual price is dinars of equal or greater value than the reported thousand.
Dinars are currency; equal or higher value means no disadvantage, so waiver stands.
5
Apply to a complex scenario
Given a scenario, determine the pre-emptors' shares and payment method, and the validity of a waiver.
Use all three rules in combination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَإِذَا اجْتَمَعَ الشُّفَعَاءُ فَالشُّفْعَةُ بَيْنَهُمْ عَلَى عَدَدِ رُءُوسِهِمْ بِالسَّوِيَّةِ وَلَا يُعْتَبَرُ اخْتِلَافُ الْأَمْلَاكِ وَمَنْ اشْتَرَى دَارًا بِعَرَضٍ أَخَذَهَا الشَّفِيعُ بِقِيمَتِهِ وَإِنْ اشْتَرَاهَا بِمَكِيلٍ، أَوْ مَوْزُونٍ أَخَذَهَا مِثْلِهِ وَإِنْ بَاعَ عَقَارًا بِعَقَارٍ أَخَذَ الشَّفِيعُ كُلَّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا بِقِيمَةِ الْآخَرِ ٢٠ وَإِذَا بَلَغَ الشَّفِيحَ أَنَّهَا بِيعَتْ بِأَلْفِ فَسَلَّمَ شُفْعَتَهُ، ثُمَّ عَلِمَ أَنَّهَا بِيعَتْ بِأَقَلَّ، أَوْ بِحِنْطَةٍ، أَوْ بِشَعِيرٍ قِيمَتُهُ أَلْفٌ، أَوْ أَكْثَرُ فَتَسْلِيمُهُ بَاطِلٌ وَلَهُ الشُّفْعَةُ وَإِنْ بَانَ أَنَّهَا بِيعَتْ بِدَنَانِيرَ قِيمَتُهَا ألْفٌ، أَوْ أَكْثَرُ فَلَا شُفْعَةَ لَهُ
And when multiple pre-emptors are together, the pre-emption is among them equally according to the number of heads, and difference in shares of ownership is not considered. And whoever buys a house with a commodity, the pre-emptor takes it with its value. If he buys it with a measured or weighed item, he takes it with its like. And if he sells real estate for real estate, the pre-emptor takes each one for the value of the other. And when the news reaches the pre-emptor that it was sold for a thousand, so he waives his pre-emption, then he learns that it was sold for less, or for wheat or barley whose value is a thousand or more, his waiver is void and he has pre-emption. But if it becomes apparent that it was sold for dinars whose value is a thousand or more, he has no pre-emption.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This final passage from the matn addresses three topics. First, multiple pre-emptors share equally per person, not according to their ownership shares. Second, payment types: if the price is a commodity ('araḍ), the shafi' pays its market value; if measured by volume or weight, he gives the same quantity of that type (not value); if property exchanged for property, he pays the value of each property to the respective seller. Third, a mistaken price: if the shafi' is told the price is 1000 and waives his right, then discovers the true price was less, he may retract; similarly if the price was goods worth 1000, he can retract. But if the actual price was dinars worth 1000 or more (meaning equal or higher than reported), his waiver stands.

Key Terms
عَرَض ('araḍ): A commodity other than gold, silver, or currency; goods measured non-standardly.
مَكِيلٍ أَوْ مَوْزُونٍ (makīl aw mawzūn): Items sold by volume or weight (e.g., grain, oil).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If three people have the right of shuf'ah on a property sold for 3000, but one owns 1/2, another 1/3, and the third 1/6, how do they take the property?
A) 1500, 1000, 500
B) 1000 each
C) 1000, 1000, 1000
D) According to mutual agreement

Key Takeaway
Pre-emptors share equally by head; payment mirrors the price type; mistaken low price can undo a waiver.

Five Key Rulings on Pre-emption from Al-Quduri
Matn rulings on shuf'a without proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five rulings on pre-emption from Al-Quduri's matn and apply them to simple scenarios involving waiver, agency, partial sale, sequential sales, and non-monetary payment.

Core Idea
A pre-emption right based on false information is not lost; an agent is the opponent until delivery; a token exception defeats the right; sequential sales only attach to the first share; payment method does not change the price.

This passage from the Kitab al-Shuf'a presents five distinct rulings that a jurist must memorize. Each ruling handles a specific twist: mistaken waiver, agency, partial exclusion, sequential sales, and non-monetary payment. Understanding these examples clarifies the boundaries of the pre-emption right in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five independent rulings
List each ruling: (1) waiver based on false buyer identity revives the right; (2) an agent who buys for another is the opponent (khasm) until he surrenders the property; (3) selling all except a cubit along the preemptor's boundary eliminates the right; (4) in sequential sales of a property, pre-emption attaches to the first share only; (5) if the buyer pays with a cloth (non-monetary consideration), the preemptor pays the monetary price, not the cloth.
These are all from the same matn passage.
2
For each ruling, note the condition or exception
Ruling 1: The waiver is conditional on correct identity—if the preemptor was misinformed, the waiver is void. Ruling 2: The agent is the opponent only until delivery; after that, the principal becomes the opponent. Ruling 3: The exclusion must be along the boundary that adjoins the preemptor and must be the entire sale except a negligible strip (a cubit). Ruling 4: The neighbor/co-owner gets pre-emption only on the first sale because after that, his right is considered exercised or the property is no longer undivided. Ruling 5: Pre-emption is based on the price (thaman), not the mode of payment.
Memorize the Arabic phrasing for each.
3
Apply each ruling to a simple hypothetical
For each ruling, construct a short scenario: (1) Preemptor waives after being told buyer is 'Ali, but later learns buyer is 'Umar; ruling: he may still claim. (2) Agent buys on behalf of principal; preemptor sues agent before delivery; ruling: agent is proper opponent. (3) Seller sells his entire house except a one-cubit strip along neighbor's wall; neighbor cannot preempt. (4) A sells half to B, then the other half to C; neighbor can preempt only the first half. (5) Buyer pays 1000 dinars and a sword; preemptor can take the house for 1000 dinars.
These are basic applications.
4
Memorize the exact Arabic phrasing
The matn's wording is precise and must be memorized. Read the source_arabic aloud several times, focusing on key phrases: 'فَسَلَّمَ الشُّفْعَةَ ثُمَّ عَلِمَ أَنَّهُ غَيْرُهُ فَلَهُ الشُّفْعَةُ', 'فَهُوَ الْخَصْمُ فِي الشُّفْعَةِ إِلَّا أَنْ يُسَلَّمَهَا', 'إِلَّا مِقْدَارَ ذِرَاعٍ فِي طُولِ الحَدِ الَّذِي يَلِي الشَّفِيعَ فَلَا شُفْعَةَ', 'فَالشُّفْعَةُ لِلْجَارِ فِي السَّهْمِ الْأَوَّلِ دُونَ الثَّانِي', 'فَالشُّفْعَةُ بِالثَّمَنِ دُونَ الثَّوْبِ'.
Highlight the exceptions: except (إلا) and condition (إذا).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كِتَابُ الشُّفْعَةِ
وَإِذَا قِيلَ لَهُ: إِنَّ الْمُشْتَرِي فُلانٌ فَسَلَّمَ الشُّفْعَةَ، ثُمَّ عَلِمَ أَنَّهُ غَيْرُهُ فَلَهُ الشُّفْعَةُ وَمَنْ اشْتَرَى دَارًا لِغَيْرِهِ فَهُوَ الْخَصْمُ فِي الشُّفْعَةِ إِلَّا أَنْ يُسَلَّمَهَا إِلَى الْمُوَكَّلِ وَإِذَا بَاعَ دَارِهِ إِلَّا مِقْدَارَ ذِرَاعٍ فِي طُولِ الحَدِ الَّذِي يَلِي الشَّفِيعَ فَلَا شُفْعَةَ لَهُ ۲۲ وَإِنْ بَاعَ سَهْمًا مِنْهَا بِثَمَنِ، ثُمَّ بَاعَ بَقِيَّتَهَا فَالشُّفْعَةُ لِلْجَارِ فِي السَّهْمِ الْأَوَّلِ دُونَ الثَّانِي وَإِنْ ابْتَاعَ بِثَمَنِ، ثُمَّ دَفَعَ إِلَيْهِ ثَوْبًا فَالشُّفْعَةُ بِالثَّمَنِ دُونَ الثَّوْبِ
The Book of Pre-emption. And if it is said to him: 'The buyer is so-and-so,' and he waives the pre-emption, then he learns that it was someone else, he has the pre-emption. And whoever buys a house for another, he is the opponent in pre-emption, unless he surrenders it to the principal. And if he sells his house except the measure of a cubit in length of the boundary that adjoins the preemptor, then there is no pre-emption for him. And if he sells a share of it for a price, then he sells the remainder, the pre-emption belongs to the neighbor in the first share, not in the second. And if he purchases for a price, then pays him a cloth, the pre-emption is for the price, not for the cloth.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage opens the Book of Pre-emption with a series of rulings from the matn. First, if a preemptor is told the identity of the buyer and waives his right, but discovers it was a different person, his right revives—because his waiver was based on misinformation, not a true relinquishment. Second, an agent who buys for someone else is considered the direct party in the pre-emption lawsuit (khasm) until he transfers the property to his principal; after delivery, the principal becomes the proper opponent. Third, if a seller excludes a mere cubit (about 0.5 m) of the side facing the preemptor, the sale is considered partial and the preemptor has no right—since pre-emption attaches to the whole property, not to a negligible remainder. Fourth, if the seller sells one share of a jointly owned property and later sells the rest, the neighbor (who is a co-sharer in the property) only has pre-emption for the first share, because the second sale is between the seller and another, and the neighbor's right was already exercised or waived. Fifth, if the buyer pays the seller with a cloth (non-monetary consideration), the preemptor can still assert pre-emption by paying the monetary equivalent, not the cloth—because pre-emption is based on the price, not the mode of payment.

Key Terms
شُفْعَة (shuf'a): Pre-emption: the right of a co-owner or neighbor to purchase a sold property by paying its price to the buyer.
خَصْم (khasm): Opponent in a lawsuit; the person against whom the preemptor claims his right.
وَلِي (yali): Adjacent to; the side of the property that touches the preemptor's boundary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a preemptor waives the shuf'a after being told the buyer is Zayd, but later learns the actual buyer was 'Amr, what is the ruling?
A) His waiver stands; he has no recourse.
B) He has the right to claim shuf'a again because the information was false.
C) He can only claim if the price changed.
D) He can claim but must compensate the buyer for the delay.

Key Takeaway
Pre-emption rights are nuanced; waiver is invalid under false pretences, and the right attaches to the first sale only when multiple sales occur.

The Legal Device (Hila) to Drop Pre-emption: Abu Yusuf vs. Muhammad
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the permissibility of using legal tricks to nullify pre-emption.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad on the permissibility of using a hila to drop pre-emption and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Using a legal trick to drop pre-emption is not disliked according to Abu Yusuf but disliked according to Muhammad.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute on the moral status of legal devices (hiyal) in property transactions. Pre-emption (shuf'a) is a right of a co-owner to purchase a sold share. Can a seller and buyer circumvent this right through a lawful stratagem? Two great Hanafi imams differ.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
What is the legal device (hila) intended to achieve? It aims to extinguish the right of pre-emption (shuf'a) for the co-owner.
The hila must be technically valid; the dispute is about its moral status.
2
State Abu Yusuf's ruling and reasoning
Abu Yusuf holds that using a hila to drop pre-emption is not disliked (la tukrah). His reasoning: since the pre-emptor can voluntarily waive his right, any lawful means achieving the same end is permissible.
This is the mufta bihi position in the later school.
3
State Muhammad's ruling and reasoning
Muhammad al-Shaybani considers such a hila makruh (disliked). He sees it as a form of deception (khida') that deprives the pre-emptor of a right, even if the contract is technically valid.
Muhammad's view is one of religious caution (taqwa).
4
Identify the practical difference
The thamarah al-khilaf: In practice, a seller and buyer may follow Abu Yusuf and use the hila without sin, but if they follow Muhammad, they would avoid it out of piety.
Both positions are considered valid ijtihad within the Hanafi school.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
Later Hanafi fatwa works often prefer Abu Yusuf's view, as pre-emption is a right that can be waived, and the device is a valid contract.
However, if the pre-emptor suspects fraud, he may still claim his right through the court.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلا تُكْرَهُ الحِيلَةُ فِي إِسْقَاطِ الشُّفْعَةِ عِند أبي يُوسُفَ وَيُكْرَهُ عِنْدَ مُحَمَّدٍ
And the legal device for dropping pre-emption is not disliked according to Abu Yusuf, but it is disliked according to Muhammad.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. The issue is whether it is permissible (or makruh) for the seller and buyer to use a legal stratagem (hila) to circumvent the pre-emption right. For example, the seller might gift the property or sell it with a condition that the buyer will resell to a third party, thereby extinguishing the pre-emptor's right. Abu Yusuf holds that since the shuf'a is a right that can be waived by the preemptor, any lawful means to eliminate it is not objectionable (makruh). Muhammad al-Shaybani, on the other hand, considers such tricks makruh tanzihan (religiously disliked) because they involve manipulation to deprive someone of their established right, even if technically valid. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the later school is closer to Abu Yusuf, but Muhammad's view is also respected as a caution. The text does not present a proof, only the positions.

Key Terms
حِيلَة (hila): A legal stratagem or device used to achieve a permissible end through seemingly permissible means, often to avoid an obligation or right.
إِسْقَاط (isqat): Dropping or extinguishing a right, here the pre-emption right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad, using a legal device to drop pre-emption is:
A) Permissible (mubah)
B) Disliked (makruh)
C) Forbidden (haram)
D) Recommended (mustahabb)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has an internal dispute on the moral status of using legal tricks to drop pre-emption: Abu Yusuf permits, Muhammad dislikes, and the relied-upon position is Abu Yusuf's.
